## T7 — Lexical, Literary, and Scientific Analysis (20 prompts)

---

### T7

#### T7.1 — Lexical and Semantic Analysis

##### T7.1.1 — What are the primary Hebrew and Greek terms for this characteristic — and what do their root meanings reveal?

**[CHAR-1] Ritual defilement-state** (E)

Three terms carry Characteristic 1. H2930A (ta.me, verb, root ṭ-m-ʾ): "to be unclean, to defile" — the root conveys contamination, the condition of being ritually or morally impure, with the basic sense of a thing made unfit through contact or condition. H2931 (ta.me, adjective, same root): "unclean" — the stative form naming the condition as a property of the person. H5079 (nid.dah, from nādad — "to flee, to wander"): "impurity, menstrual impurity, uncleanness" — the root meaning of separation/withdrawal may encode the social dynamic of the defilement-state (the defiled person is one who is separated from). G0169 (akathartos, alpha-privative + katharos — "clean, pure"): "unclean, impure" — the Greek term is simply the negative of the clean/pure term; it names the condition by the absence of what it lacks. The root meanings confirm the characteristic as a condition of unfitness/contamination (Hebrew) and the absence of the positive state (Greek).

**[CHAR-2] Moral-inner defilement-state** (E)

Five terms carry Characteristic 2 (see §2). H2931 (ta.me adj, root ṭ-m-ʾ): "unclean" — in this characteristic's Hebrew metaphorical register (Job 14:4, Isa 6:5, Isa 64:6, Hag 2:14), the root meaning of contamination/unfitness is applied to the inner person's constitutional moral condition rather than to a ritual-bodily state. G0167 (akatharsia, alpha-privative + katharos — "clean/pure"): "impurity" — the noun naming the absence of moral purity in the inner person; the root reveals it as the negative of the clean/pure state. G3435 (molunō, from the same root as "smear, stain, soil"): "to defile, soil" — the active verb of the defiling act on the inner person or garment; the root suggests physical smearing applied metaphorically to the moral inner person. G3436 (molusmos, from molunō): "defilement" — the noun form of the soiling act. G3394 (miasmos, from miainō — "to stain, pollute"): "defilement" — the staining/pollution image applied to the inner person's defiled condition.

**[CHAR-3] Corporate/covenantal defilement** (E)

Three terms carry Characteristic 3, all Hebrew (no NT Greek terms appear in the 83-verse corpus). H2930A (ta.me verb, 63V, root ṭ-m-ʾ): "to be/become unclean, to defile" — the dominant term, operating here primarily in the hiphil (causative) and qal forms: the community defiles the land (Jer 2:7: "you defiled my land"), the sanctuary (Eze 5:11), the divine name (Eze 43:7/8), and themselves (Eze 20:7: "do not defile yourselves"). The root conveys contamination — something has been rendered unfit, rendered repugnant, rendered incompatible with holiness. H2931 (ta.me adj, 11V): "unclean" — the stative form describing the resulting condition of persons, places, and names (Eze 22:5: "your name is defiled"; Lam 4:15: "Unclean!"). H5079 (nid.dah, 9V, from nādad — "to flee, to wander"): "impurity/menstrual impurity" — used metaphorically in this characteristic's register for the community's corporate moral repugnance before God (Eze 36:17: "like the uncleanness of a woman in her menstrual impurity"; Lam 1:17: "a filthy thing").

**[CHAR-4] Defilement by external spiritual agency** (E)

One Greek term carries this characteristic exclusively: G0169 akathartos (21V, alpha-privative + katharos — "not clean/pure"), always appearing as the modifier of pneuma ("unclean spirit" — pneuma akatharton). The root katharos conveys cleanness, purity, freedom from contamination. The alpha-privative negation names the absence of that cleanness. In Characteristic 4, akathartos operates exclusively as the modifier of pneuma: the spirit is fundamentally characterised by the absence of holiness. The term names the agent's character (unclean) rather than the condition it produces in the person — a definitional feature unique to Characteristic 4 within the M10c cluster.

---

##### T7.1.2 — What is the grammatical range of the primary term (noun, verb, adjective, participle) — and what does that range reveal about how the characteristic operates?

**[CHAR-1] Ritual defilement-state** (E)

The ta.me root operates as both verb (H2930A: "to become unclean, to defile") and adjective (H2931: "unclean"). This verb/adjective pairing reveals the characteristic as having both a dynamic and a static dimension: the verb captures the event of acquisition (the person becomes unclean), while the adjective captures the condition as a property (the person is unclean). The verb form also appears in causative constructions (hiphil: "to make/render unclean"), showing that the condition can be produced in another through one's own actions. G0169 akathartos in the 133-verse corpus operates predominantly as an adjective (describing the person or thing as unclean) — consistent with the characteristic's status as a condition. The full grammatical range shows the characteristic as a condition (adjective) that is dynamically acquired (verb) and potentially transmitted (causative).

**[CHAR-2] Moral-inner defilement-state** (E)

G0167 akatharsia (primary NT noun) operates exclusively as a noun throughout the 26-verse corpus, naming the condition as a state. G3435 molunō operates as a verb (1Cor 8:7 — "is defiled"; Rev 3:4 — "have not soiled") — capturing the act of defiling or the condition of being defiled. G3436 molusmos is a noun (2Cor 7:1 — "every defilement"). H2931 operates as an adjective (ta.me adj — "unclean" as a predicate or attributive). The range across noun (state/condition), verb (act of defiling or incurring defilement), and adjective (property of the person) reveals the characteristic as a condition (noun/adjective) that is dynamically acquired or maintained through specific acts (verb) — the same grammatical range as Characteristic 1, confirming the shared lexical architecture.

**[CHAR-3] Corporate/covenantal defilement** (E)

H2930A ta.me operates in both qal (intransitive: "become unclean" — Lev 18:20: "make yourself unclean with her") and piel/hiphil forms (causative: "defile/render unclean" — Jer 7:30: "they have set... to defile it"; Eze 5:11: "you have defiled my sanctuary"). This verbal range reveals the characteristic as operating in both directions: the community undergoes defilement (qal — passive reception of the condition) and actively defiles sacred objects and spaces (piel/hiphil — causative action directed outward). The community is both subject (defiling themselves) and agent (defiling land, sanctuary, name). This bidirectional verbal grammar is the linguistic foundation of the characteristic's centrifugal movement: the community's inner moral corruption goes outward to contaminate the sacred environment.

**[CHAR-4] Defilement by external spiritual agency** (E)

G0169 akathartos operates exclusively as an adjective throughout the 21-verse corpus — never as a noun, verb, or participle. It modifies pneuma in every verse: "unclean spirit" (pneuma akatharton — nominative, accusative, dative, genitive forms). The exclusive adjectival range reveals the characteristic as defined by the character of the defiling agent: the term describes what the spirit is (unclean), not what the person does or is. This grammatical range is distinctive within the M10c cluster: Characteristics 1-3 use the ta.me verb (H2930A) as their primary term capturing the act of defiling or becoming defiled; Characteristic 4 uses akathartos as a purely adjectival descriptor of the external agent's character.

---

##### T7.1.3 — What is the semantic range of the primary term — across what breadth of meaning does it operate?

**[CHAR-1] Ritual defilement-state** (E)

The ta.me root operates across the full defilement domain: ritual bodily defilement (Characteristic 1 — Lev 11-15 corpus), moral-inner defilement (Characteristic 2 — Hebrew metaphorical uses), corporate-covenantal defilement (Characteristic 3 — Eze idolatry corpus), and the nid.dah term extends into both ritual (Lev 12, 15) and corporate-prophetic (Eze 36:17) contexts. Within Characteristic 1's 133-verse scope, the semantic range covers: death-contact defilement (Num 19 corpus), bodily discharge defilement (Lev 15 corpus), animal categorical defilement (Lev 11 corpus), disease-verdict defilement (Lev 13-14 corpus), and object/structure categorical defilement (Lev 13:51-14:45). The range is comprehensive across the ritual domain and has extensions into moral and corporate domains via shared vocabulary.

**[CHAR-2] Moral-inner defilement-state** (E)

G0167 akatharsia in the NT corpus ranges across: (1) the inner moral state of impurity hidden beneath outward appearance (Mat 23:27, VCG-04 — "full of... all uncleanness"); (2) the condition to which desire leads when given reign (Rom 1:24, VCG-02 — heart-lust producing impurity); (3) the prior-master condition of slavery (Rom 6:19, VCG-02 — "slaves to impurity"); (4) a listing item in flesh-works (Gal 5:19, VCG-02); (5) the practicing condition that should have produced repentance but has not (2Cor 12:21, VCG-02); (6) the inner-identity incompatibility with saintly identity (Eph 5:3, VCG-04); (7) a motivational contaminant of apostolic appeal (1Th 2:3, VCG-02); (8) the condition the divine calling opposes (1Th 4:7, VCG-02); (9) the earthly drive to be mortified (Col 3:5, VCG-02). The range is entirely moral-inner: desire → condition → slavery → practice → identity-incompatibility → motivational contamination → opposition to divine calling.

**[CHAR-3] Corporate/covenantal defilement** (E)

H2930A ta.me in Characteristic 3's 63-verse corpus ranges across: sexual defilement of persons (Lev 18:20/23; Num 5:13-29; Gen 34), idolatrous self-defilement (Eze 20:7/18/30/31; Eze 23:7), sanctuary defilement (Eze 5:11; Jer 7:30; Jer 32:34), land defilement (Jer 2:7; Eze 36:17/18; Num 35:34), name defilement (Eze 43:7/8), reforming defilement of idolatrous sites (2Ki 23:8/10/13/16 — the inverse-positive use), Nazirite consecration-defilement (Num 6:7/9/12), and exile as consequence (Eze 4:14; Hos 9:4). The range is the full scope of the corporate/covenantal domain: every relational and spatial element of the covenant community's life can be defiled through the ta.me mechanism.

**[CHAR-4] Defilement by external spiritual agency** (E)

Within the 21-verse corpus, akathartos operates in one semantic register: the character of an adversarial spiritual being. Mat 10:1 (VCG-03): "authority over unclean spirits" — category term for a class of adversarial spiritual beings. Mar 1:23 (VCG-01): "a man with an unclean spirit" — naming the inhabiting entity. Mar 3:11 (VCG-03): "whenever the unclean spirits saw him" — describing a class of beings with perceptive and self-aware adversarial characteristics. The semantic range is narrower in Characteristic 4 than in Characteristics 1 and 2; here akathartos is exclusively the character-descriptor of a spiritual agent category.

---

##### T7.1.4 — Does the vocabulary include terms that distinguish distinct aspects of this characteristic — disposition versus act, received versus given, condition versus quality?

**[CHAR-1] Ritual defilement-state** (E)

Yes. The verb (H2930A ta.me) captures the act of acquisition or the event of becoming defiled; the adjective (H2931 ta.me) captures the condition or quality of being defiled. This verb/adjective distinction is the primary vocabulary distinction within the characteristic. H5079 nid.dah captures a specific sub-type (menstrual/physiological separation-impurity) that is not fully synonymous with general ta.me. G0169 akathartos in the NT categorical register (Act 10:14/28, 1Cor 7:14) captures a covenantal-status quality (categorically unclean as a determination, not only a bodily condition), distinguishing itself from the Levitical bodily mechanism uses.

**[CHAR-2] Moral-inner defilement-state** (E)

Yes. The H2931 adjective (applied to the inner constitutional condition — Job 14:4, Isa 6:5, Isa 64:6) and G0167 akatharsia (noun for the habitual-moral condition) name the state dimension. G3435 molunō (verb) names the act-dimension of defiling or incurring defilement. G3436 molusmos (noun from molunō) names the defilement in a body-and-spirit register (2Cor 7:1), emphasising constitutional penetration. G3394 miasmos (noun from miainō) names the defilement in a passion-driven context (2Pe 2:10) — emphasising the vehicle of desire. The vocabulary thus distinguishes: constitutional state (H2931), habitual moral condition (G0167), act of defiling (G3435), constitutional penetration (G3436), passion-vehicle defilement (G3394).

**[CHAR-3] Corporate/covenantal defilement** (E)

Yes. H2930A (verb) captures the act of defiling and the event of acquiring the defilement-state. H2931 (adj) captures the resulting condition — the object or person is in the state of being unclean. H5079 (nid.dah) captures the metaphorical-extended use: the bodily-impurity paradigm applied to the community's corporate moral condition, conveying the particular quality of moral repugnance (something that provokes revulsion — Eze 36:17) rather than merely describing a category-condition. The nid.dah term thus adds an affective/qualitative dimension to the corporate defilement that the ta.me vocabulary alone does not capture: the community's condition is not merely categorically defiled but actively repugnant to holiness.

**[CHAR-4] Defilement by external spiritual agency** (E)

Yes, partially. The compound phrase "the spirit of an unclean demon" (pneuma daimoniou akathartou — Luk 4:33, VCG-01) intensifies by pairing akathartos with daimonion (demon), creating a doubly-identified entity: a "spirit," "unclean," and a "demon." This compound naming distinguishes the inhabitant (the spirit) from its moral-character quality (unclean) and its category of being (daimonion). However, the vocabulary does not develop distinct terms for the condition produced in the person versus the character of the agent — the 21-verse corpus focuses almost entirely on naming the agent.

---

##### T7.1.5 — Does the vocabulary include a term for the structural opposite or absence of this characteristic?

**[CHAR-1] Ritual defilement-state** (E)

Yes. H2889 (tahor, "clean, pure") and H2893 (tohorah, "cleanness, purification") are the Hebrew structural opposites — these belong to the M12 (Purity) cluster as its primary vocabulary. G2513 (katharos, "clean, pure") is the Greek structural opposite, appearing in the binary clean/unclean pairs (Act 10:14/28: "common or unclean"; 1Cor 7:14: "holy rather than unclean"). The structural opposite vocabulary is consistently present alongside the defilement-state vocabulary throughout the 133-verse corpus — the binary pair always appears together, confirming that the defilement-state is understood only in relation to its opposite.

**[CHAR-2] Moral-inner defilement-state** (E)

Yes. The structural opposite is carried by the katharos/hagios vocabulary of M12 (Purity): 1Th 4:7 (VCG-02) — "holiness" (hagiasmos) as the opposite of akatharsia; 2Cor 7:1 (VCG-03) — "holiness" (hagiōsunē) as the goal of self-purification from defilement; Rev 3:4 (VCG-05) — the garments "not soiled" (the absence of molunō) as the positive state; Isa 35:8 (VCG-05) — "Way of Holiness" (the positive alternative to which the unclean are excluded). The structural opposite vocabulary is primarily the holiness/purity cluster (M12), present throughout the corpus.

**[CHAR-3] Corporate/covenantal defilement** (E)

Yes. The structural opposite is carried by H2889 tahor (clean/pure) and the holiness vocabulary (qādôsh — holy). Both are present in the corpus as explicit structural contrasts: Eze 22:26 (VCG-05) — "they have made no distinction between the holy and the common... the unclean and the clean" — the tahor/tame and qādôsh/chol pairs are named together. Eze 44:23 (VCG-05) — "they shall teach my people the difference between the holy and the common, and show them how to distinguish between the unclean and the clean." The structural opposite is not an additional isolated term but a systematic binary pair (clean/unclean, holy/common) that governs the entire characteristic's domain.

**[CHAR-4] Defilement by external spiritual agency** (E)

Yes. The structural opposite is present through the healing/wholeness vocabulary (M12): Luk 6:18 (VCG-02) — "were cured" (iathēsan — from iaomai, to heal/restore to wholeness); Luk 9:42 (VCG-01) — "healed" (iasato); Act 5:16 (VCG-02) — "all healed" (etherapeuonto). The healing vocabulary names the structural opposite in its dynamic form — the act of restoration to wholeness. The adjective katharos (clean/pure) is the implicit structural opposite at the root level.

---

##### T7.1.6 — Does the vocabulary include a person-type term — a term for the one who habitually possesses or exercises this characteristic?

**[CHAR-1] Ritual defilement-state** (S)

The 133-verse corpus does not include a distinct person-type term for the habitual bearer of the ritual defilement-state. The vocabulary uses the condition-descriptors ("the one who is unclean," "the one with the discharge," "the leprous person") but not a fixed noun naming a type of person defined by the characteristic. The disease registers come closest: "the leprous man" (Lev 13:44-45, M10c-B-VCG-02/03) is a quasi-person-type, but it is a contextual description, not a vocabulary term.

**[CHAR-2] Moral-inner defilement-state** (E)

Yes, partially. Eph 5:5 (VCG-04): "everyone who is... impure" (akathartos) — this is a person-type designation: the impure person defined by their identity in the characteristic. 2Pe 2:10 (VCG-03): those who "indulge in the lust of defiling passion" — a person-type characterised by their pattern of practice. Mat 23:27 (VCG-04): "scribes and Pharisees, hypocrites" — a named person-type whose defining inner character is moral-inner defilement masked by outward purity. The vocabulary includes contextual person-type designations without a fixed noun-term for the habitually morally impure person.

**[CHAR-3] Corporate/covenantal defilement** (E)

Partially. Lam 4:15 (VCG-05) — "Unclean! Away!" — names the exiles as a collective person-type: those publicly declared unclean and expelled. The ta.me adjective functions as a quasi-person-type descriptor: "everyone who is unclean through contact with the dead" (Num 5:2, Characteristic 1 register, but analogous in form to the characteristic's corporate designation). Within the 83-verse corpus, the nearest person-type is the corporate Israel as "the house of Israel" in a state of defilement (Eze 14:11: "the house of Israel may no more go astray from me, nor defile themselves"). No fixed noun-term for the habitually corporately defiling community exists in the corpus.

**[CHAR-4] Defilement by external spiritual agency** (E)

Partially. Act 5:16 (VCG-02): "those afflicted with unclean spirits" (ochleoumenoi — present participle of ochleō — to be troubled/afflicted) — names a class of persons defined by their affliction. Luk 6:18 (VCG-02): "those troubled with unclean spirits" (same verb in participial form) — a person-type of the afflicted. These participial forms are the closest the vocabulary comes to person-type terms. There is no fixed noun-term for the habitually possessed person in the 21-verse corpus.

---

##### T7.1.7 — Does the vocabulary include a supplication or seeking term — a term for the act of seeking this characteristic from another?

**[CHAR-1] Ritual defilement-state** (S)

The defilement-state is not sought — it is incurred or declared. There is no supplication vocabulary for receiving the defilement-state from another. The seeking that does appear in the corpus is the seeking of remedy: Num 9:7 (M10c-A-VCG-07) — the men approach Moses to ask about provision for their defilement. This is a supplication for restoration, not for the characteristic itself. The vocabulary of seeking belongs to the M11 (Cleansing) domain, not to the defilement-state itself.

**[CHAR-2] Moral-inner defilement-state** (S)

The 26-verse corpus does not include a supplication or seeking term for Characteristic 2. The defilement-state is not sought from another; the calls to cleansing (2Cor 7:1, VCG-03) are calls to active self-purification, not to petition of another for the characteristic. No seeking vocabulary is present.

**[CHAR-3] Corporate/covenantal defilement** (S)

The 83-verse corpus does not include a supplication or seeking term for the corporate/covenantal defilement-state. The characteristic is not sought; it is incurred through moral defection. The seeking that does appear is the seeking of divine inquiry (Eze 20:31 — "shall I be inquired of by you?" — the community seeking God while in the defilement-state, which God refuses). The seeking vocabulary relates to attempting to approach God while defiled, not to seeking the defilement itself.

**[CHAR-4] Defilement by external spiritual agency** (E)

Yes — for the resolution, not for the condition. Mar 7:25 (VCG-01): "came and fell down at his feet" (prosepesen pros tous podas autou — prostrating in urgent supplication) — the mother's seeking of healing. Act 5:16 (VCG-02): "bringing the sick and those afflicted" — the verb "bringing" (pheron) captures the communal act of bringing the afflicted as a form of proxy supplication. The seeking vocabulary is oriented toward the resolution (seeking healing) rather than the condition itself.

---

##### T7.1.8 — What does the relationship between the OT Hebrew vocabulary and the NT Greek vocabulary reveal about continuity or development of this characteristic across the Testaments?

**[CHAR-1] Ritual defilement-state** (E)

Substantial continuity with a significant point of revision. Continuity: G0169 akathartos in the NT carries the same clean/unclean binary logic as H2931 ta.me in the OT — the categorical structure is preserved. The NT akathartos in the M10c-B-VCG-05 corpus (Act 10:14, 11:8) shows Peter operating with OT categorical logic fully intact. Revision: Act 10:28 shows the categorical system being authoritatively revised: "God has shown me that I should not call any person common or unclean." The divine speech act revises the OT categorical determination at the person-level. 1Cor 7:14 shows a further development: "unclean" is applied to a relational-covenantal status (children of unbelievers) rather than to bodily condition. The NT development moves from primarily bodily/ritual origin to including categorical-conscience and relational-covenantal origins.

**[CHAR-2] Moral-inner defilement-state** (E)

The Hebrew-to-Greek vocabulary arc reveals a development from symbol-and-law to inner-reality. The Hebrew H2931 ta.me adj in Characteristic 2's VCG-01 corpus (Job 14:4, Isa 6:5, Isa 64:6, Hag 2:14) names the inner-moral uncleanness within a legal-ritual vocabulary that also governs Characteristic 1. The Hebrew prophetic use already makes the metaphorical-inner move (Isa 6:5 — "unclean lips" as a moral-inner recognition; Isa 64:6 — "all have become like the unclean") — the inner-moral move is already present in the OT. The NT Greek vocabulary (G0167 akatharsia, G3435 molunō, G3436 molusmos, G3394 miasmos) develops this inner-moral register explicitly and comprehensively, applying it to the conscience (1Cor 8:7), the will (Rom 6:19), the heart (Rom 1:24), and the body-and-spirit constitution (2Cor 7:1). The development is from occasional Hebrew prophetic inner-register to systematic NT moral-inner register.

**[CHAR-3] Corporate/covenantal defilement** (E)

Characteristic 3's 83-verse corpus is entirely OT Hebrew — no NT Greek verses appear. This is analytically significant in itself: the corporate/covenantal defilement-state is primarily an OT register, reflecting the specific covenantal structure of Israel-land-sanctuary-name that the OT context provides. The NT does not develop a direct corporate/covenantal defilement register using this vocabulary — Characteristic 3's concern (land, sanctuary, divine name as objects of defilement through corporate moral defection) is handled differently in the NT (through ecclesiology, the body of Christ as temple, the name of Jesus being profaned). The OT-only vocabulary confirms Characteristic 3 as the most distinctively OT of the four M10c characteristics, tied to the specific covenantal-sacral structures of Israel's life.

**[CHAR-4] Defilement by external spiritual agency** (E)

Characteristic 4 has no OT Hebrew vocabulary in its 21-verse corpus — it is entirely NT Greek. This is analytically significant: the unclean-spirit defilement register is a distinctly NT phenomenon in this programme's evidence base. The Hebrew ta.me root that carries Characteristics 1, 2, and 3 is absent from Characteristic 4's evidence. This OT-to-NT discontinuity reveals Characteristic 4 as the register that the NT gospel narrative introduces as its distinctive contribution to the defilement domain: the confrontation between Christ's authority and adversarial spiritual beings inhabiting and defiling persons.

---

##### T7.1.9 — Is there a term newly coined in the NT period for this characteristic — and if so, what does that coinage reveal?

**[CHAR-1] Ritual defilement-state** (S)

No NT neologism is evidenced for this characteristic in the 133-verse corpus. G0169 akathartos is the standard Hellenistic Greek term for "unclean," not a NT coinage. The characteristic's NT vocabulary is a continuation of existing Greek terms applied to the categorical system inherited from the OT. No new term was needed because the category itself was being revised rather than named differently.

**[CHAR-2] Moral-inner defilement-state** (E)

G3394 miasmos (2Pe 2:10, VCG-03) and G3436 molusmos (2Cor 7:1, VCG-03) appear rarely in the NT and their use in the inner-moral register (particularly molusmos at 2Cor 7:1 — "defilement of body and spirit") represents a development of the application of the defilement vocabulary to the full inner-outer constitution. G3436 molusmos specifically naming "defilement" in the body-and-spirit pairing is not a new coinage (the term exists in Greek literature) but its application to name the inner-spiritual condition of the person as a whole is the NT's distinctive move. No entirely new coinage is evidenced, but the molusmos body-and-spirit application is the NT's most distinctive terminological development for the characteristic.

**[CHAR-3] Corporate/covenantal defilement** (S)

No NT terms appear in the 83-verse corpus. Characteristic 3 is an entirely OT-Hebrew vocabulary phenomenon within this programme's evidence base. No NT coinage is evidenced.

**[CHAR-4] Defilement by external spiritual agency** (E)

The compound phrase pneuma akatharton ("unclean spirit") as a technical term for a class of adversarial spiritual beings inhabiting human persons is the NT's distinctive coinage. While akathartos existed in Greek as a general adjective, its consistent use as a fixed compound with pneuma to name a class of adversarial spiritual agents is a NT development. The coinage reveals the NT's specific need to name the phenomenon of personal spiritual inhabitation by adversarial agents as a category requiring authoritative response.

---

##### T7.1.10 — What does the full vocabulary arc reveal about this characteristic's complete semantic range?

**[CHAR-1] Ritual defilement-state** (E)

The full vocabulary arc (H2930A verb → H2931 adjective → H5079 nid.dah → G0169 akathartos) reveals the characteristic as: (1) a dynamic event (verb — acquisition) and a static condition (adjective — state); (2) a paradigm-case bodily impurity (nid.dah — the menstrual/separation paradigm governing all bodily-discharge impurity); (3) a categorical conviction operative in conscience (akathartos in the NT categorical register). The arc reveals that the characteristic spans from the most granular bodily-mechanism level (specific discharge types, specific contact pathways) through the categorical-systemic level (the comprehensive clean/unclean taxonomy) to the conscience-formation level (the inner conviction that shapes the person's categorical perception of persons and things). The full semantic range is bodily → categorical → conscience.

**[CHAR-2] Moral-inner defilement-state** (E)

The vocabulary arc (H2931 metaphorical-inner → G0167 akatharsia → G3435 molunō → G3436 molusmos → G3394 miasmos) reveals the characteristic's complete semantic range as: (1) constitutional human moral uncleanness before God (H2931 — inherited condition); (2) the habitual moral inner condition of impurity as a prior-master over the will (G0167 akatharsia); (3) the act of soiling or defiling the inner moral integrity (G3435 molunō); (4) the defilement as a penetrating condition of body and spirit (G3436 molusmos — 2Cor 7:1); (5) the defilement arising from passion-driven desire combined with contempt for authority (G3394 miasmos — 2Pe 2:10). The full arc: constitutional → habitual-dispositional → act-of-defiling → constitutional-penetration → passion-contempt complex. The range encompasses the characteristic from its deepest origin (constitutional inherited condition) to its most specific articulation (passion-driven defilement of the will).

**[CHAR-3] Corporate/covenantal defilement** (E)

The vocabulary arc (H2930A ta.me verb → H2931 ta.me adj → H5079 nid.dah) reveals the characteristic's complete semantic range as: (1) the act of defiling (ta.me verb — both self-defilement and external defilement of sacred spaces); (2) the resulting condition of being defiled (ta.me adj — persons, names, spaces declared unclean); (3) the quality of moral repugnance that the defilement-state generates in God's perception (nid.dah — the community's conduct as morally revolting as menstrual impurity). The full arc: act → condition → repugnance. The characteristic is thus not merely a legal category (defiled/not defiled) but a moral-relational reality that produces divine revulsion (Eze 36:17 nid.dah metaphor) and that requires divine action (Zec 13:1 — fountain of cleansing) for resolution.

**[CHAR-4] Defilement by external spiritual agency** (E)

The vocabulary arc is narrow and focused: G0169 akathartos as the character-descriptor of an external spiritual agent (pneuma akatharton), accompanied by healing vocabulary (iaomai — cured; therapeuō — healed) naming the structural opposite in its dynamic form. The full arc reveals: the characteristic is defined entirely by the agent who produces it rather than by the condition of the person who bears it. The healing vocabulary confirms the restoration as the telos. The arc is: unclean agent → condition produced in person (inferred from narrative) → healing restoration.

---

#### T7.2 — Verse and Literary Interpretation

##### T7.2.1 — What is the function of this characteristic's primary term within its primary verse — what role does it play in the sentence and argument?

**[CHAR-1] Ritual defilement-state** (E)

The primary anchor verse for Characteristic 1 is Num 19:11 (vc_id 21481, M10c-A-VCG-07): "Whoever touches the dead body of any person shall be unclean seven days." In this sentence, the ta.me adjective (H2931, "shall be unclean") functions as the predicate — the main assertion of the verse. The sentence structure is: universal subject ("whoever") + condition trigger ("touches the dead body of any person") + predicate-condition ("shall be unclean") + duration ("seven days"). The term is the primary claim of the sentence: this is what the contact produces. The argument function: it establishes death-contact as the most severe triggering event, producing the longest standard defilement duration.

**[CHAR-2] Moral-inner defilement-state** (E)

The primary anchor verse for Characteristic 2 (identified at T7.2.5 below) is 2Cor 7:1 (vc_id 9329, G3436 molusmos): "let us cleanse ourselves from every defilement of body and spirit, bringing holiness to completion in the fear of God." Within this sentence, molusmos (defilement) functions as the object of the cleansing imperative — it is what must be removed ("from every defilement"). The term names the comprehensive condition (body and spirit) from which the person must actively cleanse themselves. The grammatical function is: object of the preposition apo (from) — the defilement is what is being moved away from in the cleansing action.

**[CHAR-3] Corporate/covenantal defilement** (E)

The primary anchor verse for Characteristic 3 is Eze 5:11 (vc_id 21497, H2930A, VCG-03): "therefore, as I live, declares the Lord God, surely, because you have defiled my sanctuary with all your detestable things and with all your abominations, therefore I will withdraw. My eye will not spare, and I will have no pity." Within this sentence, ta.me (you have defiled, piel perfect) functions as the ground of the divine consequence — it is the stated reason for divine withdrawal. The grammatical function: causal premise (because you have defiled → therefore I will withdraw). The term names the completed act of defiling the sanctuary as the decisive moral event that triggers the divine response.

**[CHAR-4] Defilement by external spiritual agency** (E)

The primary anchor verse for Characteristic 4 is Luk 8:29 (vc_id 21297, G0169, VCG-01): "For he had commanded the unclean spirit to come out of the man. (For many a time it had seized him. He was kept under guard and bound with chains and shackles, but he would break the bonds and be driven by the demon into the desert.)" Within this verse, akathartos (in "unclean spirit") functions as the identifier of the entity being commanded out — the subject of the commanding; the spirit is named as "unclean" to establish its character as the defiling agent from which the man must be freed. The term functions as a character-qualifier justifying the command.

---

##### T7.2.2 — What literary form carries the primary verse evidence (narrative, psalm, wisdom, prophecy, epistle, apocalyptic) — and what does that form require for responsible interpretation?

**[CHAR-1] Ritual defilement-state** (E)

The dominant literary form is Levitical-Numbers legal code — normative Torah legislation presented as divine speech to Moses. This form requires reading verses as normative prescriptions within a covenant context, not as narrative descriptions of individual events. The form demands: (1) reading each case as establishing a legal category (not merely describing one incident); (2) attending to the legal logic (if X, then Y — the casuistic structure); (3) recognising that the legislation presupposes a cultic and communal context (the tabernacle, the camp, the covenant community). The M10c-B-VCG-05 NT corpus (Act 10, 1Cor 7) is narrative and epistolary, requiring attention to the narrative context (Peter's vision, the question of mixed marriages) for responsible interpretation.

**[CHAR-2] Moral-inner defilement-state** (E)

The dominant literary form is Pauline epistle (Rom 1:24/6:19, 2Cor 7:1/6:17/12:21, Gal 5:19, Eph 4:19/5:3/5:5, Col 3:5, 1Th 2:3/4:7, VCG-02/03/04) — pastoral-theological letters addressing specific communities. This form requires: (1) reading each verse within the specific communal situation being addressed (the Corinthians' unrepentance at 2Cor 12:21 is specific to that community; the Ephesian pattern of Eph 4:19 is specific to the Gentile world); (2) recognising the theological argument structure (Rom 6:19 is within Paul's theology of dying and rising with Christ); (3) not universalising every specific pastoral observation into a universal principle without attention to context. The Hebrew forms (Job 14:4 — wisdom; Isa 6:5/64:6 — prophetic; Hag 2:14 — prophetic) require attention to their specific literary contexts (Job's existential speech; Isaiah's prophetic cry). Revelation (Rev 3:4, 14:4, 16:13, 17:4, 18:2) is apocalyptic, requiring symbolic-register interpretation.

**[CHAR-3] Corporate/covenantal defilement** (E)

The dominant literary form is prophetic oracle — specifically the Ezekiel judgment-oracle (Eze 5:11, 5:17; 22:3-4; 23:38; 43:7/8, VCG-03), the Ezekiel historical-review oracle (Eze 20:7/18/26/30/31/43, VCG-02), and the Hosea prophetic accusation (Hos 5:3/6:10, VCG-02). Secondary forms: legal code (Lev 18, Num 5, Deu 21/24, VCG-01), historical narrative (Gen 34, 2Ki 23, 2Ch 36, VCG-01 and -02), lament poetry (Lam 1:17/4:15, VCG-05), and prophetic promise (Eze 37:23, Isa 52:1, Zec 13:1, VCG-05). The prophetic oracle form requires attending to the addressee (Israel/the house of Israel), the occasion (pre-exilic accusation or exilic retrospective), and the divine first-person speech that gives the oracle its authority. The legal code requires reading verses as normative prescriptions within the Mosaic covenant context.

**[CHAR-4] Defilement by external spiritual agency** (E)

The dominant literary form is gospel narrative — specifically the Synoptic Gospel accounts (Mark, Luke, Matthew). Secondary form: Acts narrative (Act 5:16, Act 8:7). Gospel narrative requires: (1) attending to the specific setting (synagogue — Mar 1:23; tombs — Mar 5:2; in front of a crowd — Mar 9:25) as theologically significant; (2) reading the expulsion accounts as both historical events and kingdom-theological demonstrations; (3) recognising the amazement of witnesses (Mar 1:27; Luk 4:36) as the narratively embedded interpretive response; (4) reading the commission texts (Mat 10:1, Mar 6:7, VCG-03) in their missional context as authoritative delegation.

---

##### T7.2.3 — What is the logical structure of key arguments in the verse evidence — what are the premises and conclusions?

**[CHAR-1] Ritual defilement-state** (E)

The primary logical structure throughout the Levitical corpus is: if the person performs action X (contact, ingestion, proximity) with agent Y (corpse, carcass, discharge-person), then the person acquires condition Z (unclean) for duration W (until evening / seven days) and must undergo remedy R (washing / water of impurity). The structure is: casuistic premise + condition-predicate + duration + remedy obligation. The argument at Lev 11:44 (M10c-A-VCG-04) has a different structure: divine nature premise ("I am holy") → command ("be holy") → prohibition ("do not defile yourselves"). The Num 19:20 argument (M10c-A-VCG-07): if man is unclean + does not cleanse + impurity water not applied → is unclean + cut off from assembly + has defiled the sanctuary. The Num 9:10-11 argument: if person is defiled or on long journey → still keep Passover at secondary date. The logical variety reflects the legal corpus's comprehensive case-structure.

**[CHAR-2] Moral-inner defilement-state** (E)

Three key logical structures are evidenced. (1) Rom 1:24 (VCG-02) — consequential logic: because they suppressed truth and rejected God (premise) → therefore God gave them up to impurity (conclusion). The premise is prior human moral defection; the conclusion is judicial release into deepened defilement. (2) Rom 6:19 (VCG-02) — analogical-imperative logic: just as you once were slaves to impurity (prior state) → so now present yourselves as slaves to righteousness (new calling). The argument uses the prior condition as the rhetorical measure for understanding the new calling. (3) 2Cor 7:1 (VCG-03) — promissory-imperative logic: since we have these promises (premise: divine promise of dwelling) → let us cleanse ourselves from every defilement (imperative: the appropriate response). The divine promise grounds and motivates the purification call. (4) Eph 5:5 (VCG-04) — definitional logic: everyone who is impure is an idolater (definition) → has no inheritance in the kingdom (consequence). The impure inner state is definitionally equivalent to idolatry, producing the consequence of exclusion.

**[CHAR-3] Corporate/covenantal defilement** (E)

Three key logical structures. (1) Eze 5:11 (VCG-03) — causal consequence: because you have defiled my sanctuary (premise) → therefore I will withdraw (consequence). The corporate act of defilement produces the divine response as a logical-covenantal consequence. (2) Eze 20:43 (VCG-02) — temporal-eschatological sequence: in that place (the future restoration) → you shall remember (temporal trigger) → you shall loathe yourselves (consequence). The memory produces the affect. (3) Zec 13:1 (VCG-05) — promissory: on that day (eschatological marker) → a fountain shall be opened (divine provision) → to cleanse from sin and uncleanness (result). The divine eschatological act addresses the accumulated corporate defilement.

**[CHAR-4] Defilement by external spiritual agency** (E)

Three key logical structures. (1) Mar 1:27 (VCG-03) — indicative-amazement: "he commands even the unclean spirits, and they obey him" — the spirits' obedience (premise) from which the community infers superior authority (conclusion). (2) Mat 10:1 (VCG-03) — commission-authority: Jesus gave them authority (premise) → to cast out unclean spirits and heal (commissioned action) — authority precedes and enables action. (3) Mat 12:43/Luk 11:24 (VCG-02) — consequential warning: when the spirit is expelled (condition) → it seeks re-entry → if it returns and finds the house empty (condition) → the final state is worse (conclusion) — warning that expulsion without positive filling produces a worse outcome.

---

##### T7.2.4 — What contextual setting carries the primary verse evidence (judicial, liturgical, covenantal, communal, eschatological) — and what does that setting reveal?

**[CHAR-1] Ritual defilement-state** (E)

The primary contextual setting is liturgical-covenantal: the Levitical legislation operates in the context of the divine presence dwelling in the tabernacle among Israel (Lev 15:31 — "defiling my tabernacle that is in their midst"). The clean/unclean system is the boundary management system for a community in which the holy God dwells. The Num 9 and Num 19 material operates in the wilderness-camp setting — Israel as a covenant community travelling with the divine presence. The M10c-B leprosy corpus (Lev 13-14) operates in a diagnostic/judicial setting — priestly examination as authoritative verdict. The NT akathartos material (Act 10-11) operates in a narrative-revelatory setting — divine vision overturning established categorical conviction. The settings reveal the characteristic as a condition managed within structured institutional contexts (tabernacle, priestly authority, apostolic revelation).

**[CHAR-2] Moral-inner defilement-state** (E)

The contextual settings vary by literary form. Communal-pastoral: 2Cor 7:1, 12:21 (VCG-03/02) — Paul addressing the Corinthian community's moral state and calling to cleansing. Covenantal-ethical: Eph 4:19, 5:3, 5:5 (VCG-02/04) — Paul framing the Gentile moral condition in contrast to the new-covenant community's calling. Theological-doctrinal: Rom 1:24, 6:19 (VCG-02) — systematic theological exposition of the defilement-state and its resolution in the gospel. Prophetic-confessional: Isa 6:5, 64:6, Hag 2:14 (VCG-01) — prophetic setting of divine encounter and communal confession before God. Wisdom-existential: Job 14:4 (VCG-01) — existential-wisdom setting of the human condition before God. Eschatological: Rev 3:4, 14:4, 16:13, 17:4, 18:2 (VCG-05/04) — apocalyptic eschatological revelation context. The diversity of settings reveals the characteristic as operating across the full range of biblical literary and communal contexts.

**[CHAR-3] Corporate/covenantal defilement** (E)

The contextual settings vary by literary form. Covenantal-judicial: the Levitical legal code (VCG-01 — Lev 18, Num 5, Deu 21/24) addresses the covenant community as bound by Mosaic law. Prophetic-accusatory: Ezekiel's pre-exilic judgment oracles (VCG-02 — Eze 20/22/23; VCG-03 — Eze 5/9/23/43) address the corporate defilement as the reason for the coming judgment. Exilic-lament: Lamentations (VCG-05 — Lam 1:17/4:15) addresses the post-destruction reality of Jerusalem's defilement and expulsion. Eschatological-promissory: the VCG-05 restoration texts (Eze 37:23, Isa 52:1, Zec 13:1) address the future community in the context of the coming divine restoration. The dominant setting is prophetic-covenantal: the characteristic's primary arena is the covenant community's relationship with its sacred environment in the context of covenantal accountability.

**[CHAR-4] Defilement by external spiritual agency** (E)

The primary contextual setting is the public communal context of Jesus's Galilean ministry: synagogue gatherings (Mar 1:23, Luk 4:33), public healing sessions (Luk 6:18 — "who came to hear him"; Act 5:16 — gathering from surrounding towns), and specific encounter narratives (Mar 5:2 — among the tombs; Mar 7:25 — a woman coming; Luk 9:42 — "while he was coming"). Secondary setting: the apostolic mission context (Act 5:16, Act 8:7 — Philip's ministry in Samaria). The dominant setting is public-communal encounter with divine authority in the context of Jesus's kingdom mission.

---

##### T7.2.5 — Does any verse function as the primary anchor for this characteristic — the verse that most fully and directly expresses its essential character?

**[CHAR-1] Ritual defilement-state** (E)

Yes. Lev 15:31 (vc_id 21462, M10c-A-VCG-08): "Thus you shall keep the people of Israel separate from their uncleanness, lest they die in their uncleanness by defiling my tabernacle that is in their midst." This verse most fully expresses the characteristic's essential nature because it: (1) names the condition directly ("their uncleanness"); (2) names the management imperative ("keep the people of Israel separate from their uncleanness"); (3) names the stakes of non-management (death); (4) names the reason for the stakes (the divine dwelling at the centre of the community); and (5) frames the entire system's purpose. All of the characteristic's sub-registers are governed by this verse's logic: defilement creates incompatibility with divine presence; separation-and-remedy maintain the conditions for divine habitation among the people.

**[CHAR-2] Moral-inner defilement-state** (E)

Yes: 2Cor 7:1 (vc_id 9329, G3436 molusmos, M10c-C-VCG-03). This verse is the primary anchor because it: (1) names the defilement-state explicitly and comprehensively ("every defilement of body and spirit"); (2) locates the defilement at both constitutional levels (body and spirit) — more comprehensively than any other verse in the corpus; (3) frames the active response (cleansing imperative) and the goal (bringing holiness to completion); (4) grounds the call in divine promise (fear of God); (5) in a single sentence captures the condition (defilement), the response (cleansing), the scope (body and spirit), and the telos (completed holiness). No other verse in the 26-verse corpus simultaneously names the condition, both constitutional levels, the required response, and the positive goal.

**[CHAR-3] Corporate/covenantal defilement** (E)

Yes: Eze 5:11 (vc_id 21497, H2930A, VCG-03). This verse is the primary anchor because it: (1) names the specific object of defilement ("my sanctuary") — the most sacred space in the covenant community; (2) names the act explicitly ("you have defiled") — the completed corporate act; (3) names the associated acts (detestable things and abominations — M10b co-register); (4) states the direct divine consequence (withdrawal of compassion — the covenantal bond's practical severance); and (5) in a single sentence captures the characteristic's fundamental structure: community defiles sacred space → covenant relationship is severed. The other VCGs extend the characteristic's domain (VCG-01 sexual-relational, VCG-04 land, VCG-02 idolatry) but VCG-03 and Eze 5:11 represent the most concentrated expression of the characteristic's essential nature.

**[CHAR-4] Defilement by external spiritual agency** (E)

Yes: Luk 8:29 (vc_id 21297, G0169, VCG-01). This verse is the primary anchor because it: (1) most completely describes the condition of the possessed person — will overridden ("driven by the demon"), all human restraint futile ("broke the bonds"), bodily agency lost, forced direction ("into the desert"); (2) names the persistent, repeated nature of the seizure ("for many a time it had seized him"); (3) captures the social dimension (kept under guard with chains and shackles — the community's failed attempt at management); (4) frames the divine command as the necessary response ("he had commanded the unclean spirit to come out"); (5) establishes the contrast between all human remedies (failed) and divine authority (effective).

---

##### T7.2.6 — What does the primary anchor verse reveal that no other verse reveals?

**[CHAR-1] Ritual defilement-state** (E)

Lev 15:31 reveals the existential stakes of the characteristic that no other verse makes as explicit: the defilement-state, left unmanaged, does not merely restrict the person — it threatens the person with death and the community with the loss of divine habitation. The divine dwelling "in their midst" is both the motivation for the entire purity system and its most vulnerable point. No other verse in the 133-verse corpus combines all four elements (the condition, the management imperative, the consequence of non-management, the reason for that consequence) in a single statement. The verse reveals the characteristic not as a merely ritual compliance system but as a life-or-death boundary condition for a community whose God dwells among them.

**[CHAR-2] Moral-inner defilement-state** (E)

2Cor 7:1 (VCG-03) uniquely reveals the bi-constitutional scope of the characteristic: "defilement of body and spirit." No other verse in the 26-verse corpus simultaneously locates the moral-inner defilement-state at both the bodily and spirit levels in a single formulation. Job 14:4 (VCG-01) names constitutional uncleanness but not body-and-spirit specifically. Rom 6:19 (VCG-02) names the will and bodily members but not the spirit. The body-and-spirit formulation of 2Cor 7:1 reveals the characteristic as penetrating to the deepest constitutional level (spirit) while simultaneously inhabiting the most external constitutional level (body) — making it the most constitutionally comprehensive statement of the characteristic in the corpus. It also uniquely grounds the cleansing call in the fear of God, revealing that the motive for resolution is not self-improvement but theocentric orientation.

**[CHAR-3] Corporate/covenantal defilement** (E)

Eze 5:11 (VCG-03) uniquely reveals the covenantal consequence of the characteristic's operation: divine withdrawal of compassion. "I will withdraw. My eye will not spare, and I will have no pity." No other verse in the 83-verse corpus is as direct in naming the consequence of the corporate/covenantal defilement-state at the level of the God-community covenantal bond: when the community defiles the sanctuary, the compassion that sustains the covenant relationship is withdrawn. This withdrawal — God's eye that no longer sees with pity — is the covenantal severance that makes the characteristic most theologically significant. Every other verse in the corpus describes the condition, its causes, and its consequences; Eze 5:11 names the covenantal rupture at its most interior point: the withdrawal of divine compassionate attention.

**[CHAR-4] Defilement by external spiritual agency** (E)

Luk 8:29 (VCG-01) uniquely reveals the futility of all human remedies: "kept under guard and bound with chains and shackles, but he would break the bonds." No other verse in the 21-verse corpus names the specific human attempts at containing the characteristic and their failure. This reveals the characteristic as beyond human management — the chains and shackles comprehensively ineffective. This failure distinguishes Characteristic 4 from all other M10c characteristics: Characteristic 1 has a prescribed remedy system; Characteristic 2 has a path through repentance; Characteristic 3 has a path through prophetic confrontation and divine cleansing. Characteristic 4's only effective remedy is the direct divine command.

---

#### T7.3 — Human Science Frameworks

##### T7.3.1 — Which human science framework (psychology, moral philosophy, developmental psychology, sociology, anthropology, or other) is most useful as an interpretive lens for this characteristic?

**[CHAR-1] Ritual defilement-state** (E)

Based on the verse evidence (noting that the science extract Section 4 was not uploaded for this session — this finding draws on the evidence corpus alone and is a partial answer): Cultural anthropology and the sociology of ritual purity are the most useful frameworks. Mary Douglas's analysis of pollution and taboo (Purity and Danger) is the most directly relevant scholarly framework for the Levitical clean/unclean system: her argument that "dirt is matter out of place" — that purity systems enforce social-symbolic boundaries by categorising what belongs where — illuminates why carcasses, bodily fluids, and skin disease trigger defilement in the corpus. The corpse-contact sub-register (M10c-A-VCG-07) and the disease-verdict sub-register (M10c-B-VCG-02/03) directly engage the pollution-and-taboo mechanisms Douglas describes. Developmental psychology (conscience formation, categorical moral reasoning) is secondarily useful for the M10c-B-VCG-01 discernment-formation register and the Act 10 categorical-revision evidence.

**[CHAR-2] Moral-inner defilement-state** (E)

Two frameworks are most useful. (1) Moral psychology — specifically the psychology of moral self-regulation, conscience, and moral disengagement (Albert Bandura's concept of moral disengagement is directly relevant: the mechanisms by which people disengage their moral standards from their conduct — moral justification, euphemistic labelling, displacement of responsibility — map onto the progressive callousness evidenced at Eph 4:19, VCG-02). (2) Addiction psychology — the evidence of progressive tolerance (Rom 6:19: "lawlessness leading to more lawlessness"), loss of freedom (Rom 6:19: "slaves to impurity"), active craving (Eph 4:19: "greedy to practice every kind of impurity"), and resistance to external intervention (2Cor 12:21: "have not repented") mirrors the structure of addictive craving and its self-reinforcing entrenchment. Note: science extract Section 4 not uploaded; this finding is drawn from the verse evidence alone.

**[CHAR-3] Corporate/covenantal defilement** (E)

Two frameworks are most useful. (1) Moral ecology / environmental ethics: the characteristic's distinctive feature is defilement that extends to shared sacred space (land, sanctuary) through the community's collective moral conduct. The concept of moral ecology — the idea that a community's moral character shapes and is shaped by the physical and social environment it inhabits — provides useful framing for understanding how corporate moral defection contaminates land (Eze 36:17: "they defiled it by their ways and their deeds"), making the environment unfit for habitation (Mic 2:10). (2) Sociology of collective memory and transgenerational trauma: Eze 20:43's distinctive vision of corporate memory as the mechanism of repentance is illuminated by sociological work on how communities hold and transmit the memory of collective wrongdoing. Collective memory research (Paul Connerton, Jan Assmann) examines how communities remember past moral failures and how that memory shapes present communal identity. Note: science extract Section 4 not uploaded; this finding is drawn from the verse evidence alone.

**[CHAR-4] Defilement by external spiritual agency** (E)

Two frameworks are most useful. (1) Psychiatry/neurology — the phenomenology of involuntary movement disorders, dissociative episodes, and conditions overriding the person's conscious will. The descriptions — convulsions (Luk 9:42, Mar 1:26), loss of bodily control, superhuman strength (Luk 8:29 — breaking chains), suppression of speech and hearing (Mar 9:25) — share phenomenological features with neurological and psychiatric conditions overriding voluntary motor and communicative functions. (2) Anthropology/cross-cultural studies of spirit possession — comparative study provides a descriptive framework for the social and communal dimensions: severance from community (Mar 5:2), public manifestation (Mar 1:23 in the synagogue), proxy-mediated seeking of healing (Mar 7:25, Act 5:16). Note: science extract Section 4 not uploaded; this finding is drawn from the verse evidence alone.

---

##### T7.3.2 — Where the human science framework illuminates the verse evidence — making a finding more coherent or complete — what does it reveal?

**[CHAR-1] Ritual defilement-state** (E)

The cultural-anthropological framework illuminates the categorical logic of the Levitical system: why certain animals are clean and others unclean is not arbitrary but reflects a classification system based on perceived boundary integrity (animals that fully belong to their domain — cloven hooves + cud-chewing for land animals, fins + scales for water animals — are clean; those that straddle categories are unclean). This helps explain the Lev 11 corpus (M10c-A-VCG-02) without requiring the individual rules to carry independent symbolic weight — the system's logic is categorical. The pollution framework also illuminates why disease (M10c-B-VCG-02/03) triggers defilement: disease violates bodily boundary integrity (the skin's surface — the body's boundary — is disrupted); the defilement-state marks the disruption. This coherence between the anthropological framework and the evidence strengthens the reading that the characteristic is constituted by its categorical and boundary-maintenance function.

**[CHAR-2] Moral-inner defilement-state** (E)

Moral disengagement theory illuminates the Eph 4:19 (VCG-02) progression: "become callous" names the moral self-regulation failure that moral disengagement produces; "given themselves up to sensuality, greedy to practice every kind of impurity" names the behaviour that follows moral disengagement. The moral psychology framework reveals that the callousness of Eph 4:19 is not a simple personality trait but a developmental consequence of repeated disengagement of moral standards — a process with identifiable stages (initial engagement, progressive desensitisation, full disengagement). Addiction psychology illuminates Rom 6:19 ("slaves to impurity... lawlessness leading to more lawlessness"): the escalation pattern and the slavery metaphor map precisely onto the phenomenology of addictive craving — the initial exposure leads to tolerance and escalation, and the will is experienced as bound despite the person's active pursuit of the substance/behaviour.

**[CHAR-3] Corporate/covenantal defilement** (E)

Moral ecology illuminates the Ezekiel corpus's consistent linkage between the community's inner moral orientation and the land's condition (Eze 36:17/18 VCG-04: idolatrous practice defiling the land; Jer 2:7: entering the land with inner corruption causing defilement). The idea that a community's collective moral conduct shapes the fitness of its physical environment for habitation aligns with what the evidence shows: the land becomes unfit for rest (Mic 2:10) not because of a ritual contact mechanism but because the community's collective moral character is incompatible with the land's sacred-inhabitable status. Collective memory sociology illuminates Eze 20:43 (VCG-02): the designed function of exile as a context for corporate memory is precisely what collective memory theory examines — how forced removal from one's homeland creates conditions for re-evaluating the community's history.

**[CHAR-4] Defilement by external spiritual agency** (E)

Neurological/psychiatric phenomenology illuminates the convulsion descriptions (Mar 1:26 — "convulsing him and crying out"; Luk 9:42 — "threw him to the ground and convulsed him") and the superhuman-strength phenomena (Luk 8:29 — "break the bonds"), which resemble adrenaline-driven physical feats reported in severe psychiatric episodes. Cross-cultural anthropology illuminates the social dynamics: the community's management through restraint (Luk 8:29 — chains), the social expulsion (Mar 5:2 — among the tombs), and the proxy-mediated seeking of healing (Act 5:16) are patterns that anthropological study of possession phenomena documents across cultures.

---

##### T7.3.3 — Where the verse evidence and the human science framework diverge, what does the divergence reveal?

**[CHAR-1] Ritual defilement-state** (E)

The key divergence: the cultural-anthropological framework (Douglas) treats purity systems as fundamentally social/symbolic — the sacred is the socially constructed category of what belongs in the centre. The verse evidence insists the system is grounded in the holiness of God (Lev 11:44-45, M10c-A-VCG-04: "I am the Lord your God... I am holy"). The defilement-state is not merely matter out of place within a social order; it is incompatibility with a divine reality that is not socially constructed. The stakes at Lev 15:31 ("lest they die") reveal a real, not merely symbolic, threat. The divergence reveals that while the anthropological framework illuminates the structural logic of the categorical system, it cannot account for the theological ontology that grounds it: the holy God who dwells among the people is not a social category but a living reality whose holiness generates the purity/defilement boundary as a real condition rather than a social construct.

**[CHAR-2] Moral-inner defilement-state** (E)

The primary divergence: both moral disengagement theory and addiction psychology treat the condition as susceptible to human therapeutic intervention — behaviour modification, cognitive restructuring, twelve-step programmes. The verse evidence insists on a different order of resolution: 2Cor 7:1 (VCG-03) grounds the cleansing in "the fear of God" — not in self-improvement strategies but in theocentric motivation; 1Th 4:7 (VCG-02) grounds the calling away from impurity in the divine calling itself. Rom 6:19 (VCG-02) frames the alternative to impurity-slavery as a new slavery — "slaves to righteousness" — rather than as liberation to autonomy. The verse evidence diverges from human science frameworks by insisting that the resolution requires divine action (2Cor 7:1 — the promises that ground the cleansing call are divine promises) rather than merely human therapeutic effort. The divine-relational and theocentric framing is the irreducible difference.

**[CHAR-3] Corporate/covenantal defilement** (E)

The primary divergence: moral ecology and collective memory sociology treat these as natural social phenomena — the community's moral character shapes its environment through social processes; collective memory is a cultural-psychological phenomenon. The verse evidence insists on a theological ontology that these frameworks cannot capture: Eze 5:11 — the sanctuary is God's own ("my sanctuary") and its defilement provokes divine withdrawal of compassion — a real covenantal-relational consequence that social-ecological frameworks have no category for. Eze 20:26 — God defiles the community through their own defiant gifts — a divine judicial act that social frameworks cannot accommodate. The verse evidence requires a category of real sacred space (not merely socially constructed sacred meaning) and real covenantal relationship (not merely social covenant) to make sense of the characteristic's stakes.

**[CHAR-4] Defilement by external spiritual agency** (E)

The primary divergence: psychiatric and neurological frameworks treat the phenomena as endogenous (arising from within the person's brain/nervous system) or as cultural constructs. The verse evidence insists on an external personal agent — the unclean spirit is a distinct, addressable entity: Mar 5:8 — "Come out of the man, you unclean spirit!" — addressed in the second person as a rational agent; Mar 3:11 — the spirits "cried out, 'You are the Son of God'" — they speak, perceive, and recognise. The ontological claim of the NT evidence (an external personal agent inhabiting the person) cannot be accommodated within purely naturalistic frameworks. The verse evidence's insistence on a distinct addressable spiritual agent is the irreducible divergence point.

---

##### T7.3.4 — Does the human science framework surface any aspect of this characteristic that the verse evidence has not yet addressed — and does that absence require further verse investigation?

**[CHAR-1] Ritual defilement-state** (G)

The conscience-formation aspect of the characteristic (M10c-B-VCG-01 — cultivating the discernment faculty through the purity system) connects to developmental psychology's study of moral categorical reasoning and the formation of implicit moral convictions. The Act 10:14 evidence (Peter's deeply formed categorical conviction) is a striking case of implicit bias — a conviction so embedded it resists even divine instruction. Developmental psychology and implicit cognition research might illuminate how the purity categorical system forms the person's implicit moral framework over time, and what it takes to revise such a framework. This suggests further verse investigation: are there other verses in the broader programme's corpus where the formed categorical conviction is revised or encounters a crisis? This falls outside Characteristic 1's 133-verse scope but is flagged as a gap for potential cross-characteristic investigation.

Science extract (Section 4, Defilement/Impurity): not uploaded in this session. This section of T7.3 is accordingly partial. Gap noted for researcher: the science extract finding for T7.3 should be completed once Section 4 is available.

**[CHAR-2] Moral-inner defilement-state** (E)

Attachment theory would ask: what relational bonds formed the conscience's orientation toward idol-associated categories? This is not directly addressed in the 26 verses. The absence may reflect the verse evidence's theological framing (divine calling and human will) rather than developmental-psychological framing. Whether further verse investigation is warranted: the broader programme's corpus (particularly the formation and conscience formation clusters) may address this; it is not a gap specific to Characteristic 2 alone but points to a cross-cluster developmental-psychological dimension. **[CHAR-3] Corporate/covenantal defilement** (E)

Collective memory research surfaces the question of which institutional mechanisms enable communities to remember their collective moral failures accurately — and which mechanisms enable distortion or suppression of such memory. The verse evidence (Jer 2:23 — corporate denial; Eze 20:43 — designed future memory) shows that corporate memory of defilement is both suppressed (denial) and divinely designed for future activation (Eze 20:43). But the verse evidence does not address the institutional mechanisms that enable or prevent accurate corporate memory transmission — this is a gap that collective memory sociology surfaces. Within the programme's broader corpus, study of prophetic literature's memory-forming function might address this gap. Science extract Section 4 not uploaded — this finding is partial; completion requires Section 4 when available.

**[CHAR-4] Defilement by external spiritual agency** (E)

Trauma psychology surfaces the question of long-term inner-being effects of sustained spirit-possession on the person after restoration — a dimension the verse evidence treats as resolved by healing (Luk 6:18 — "cured"; Act 5:16 — "all healed") but does not explore. What happens to the person's sense of self, their experience of their own will and bodily agency, their community trust, after sustained domination? The verse evidence does not narrate the post-restoration inner life of specific persons. Trauma psychology would ask whether the person who experienced systematic will-override, bodily subjugation, and communal severance carries residual inner-being effects even after the physical condition is reversed. Science extract Section 4 not uploaded — this finding is partial; completion requires Section 4 when available.
